  • Error Boundaries catch errors during rendering, lifecycle methods, and constructors of child components.
  • They prevent the "White Screen of Death" by providing a Fallback UI.
  • They do not catch errors in event handlers (like button clicks) or asynchronous code (like setTimeout or fetch). Use standard try/catch blocks for those.
  • They must currently be implemented as Class Components using getDerivedStateFromError.
Q58

What happens when a component unmounts?

▼

Deep Dive: React Fragments

React has a strict rule: A component must return a single top-level root element. If you try to return two adjacent <div> tags, React will throw a syntax error.

Historically, developers solved this by wrapping everything in a giant container <div>. However, this pollutes the HTML DOM with hundreds of useless, empty <div> wrappers, which severely breaks CSS layouts like Flexbox and CSS Grid (where elements must be direct children to work properly).

React Fragments (<React.Fragment> or the shorthand <> </>) solve this. They group a list of children together without adding extra nodes to the DOM.

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine you buy an Apple, a Banana, and a Pear. The cashier (React) says, "I can only scan one item." So you put them in a plastic bag (a container <div>) and hand them the bag. But now there's unnecessary plastic polluting the environment (DOM bloat). A React Fragment is like an invisible rubber band. It groups them together for the cashier to scan, but magically disappears as soon as you walk out the store, leaving only the fruits.

jsx 
// ❌ BAD: Polluting DOM with useless wrappers
function BadComponent() {
  return (
    <div>  {/* This div breaks CSS Flexbox layouts! */}
      <td>Column 1</td>
      <td>Column 2</td>
    </div>
  );
}

// ✅ GOOD: Using Fragment Shorthand
function GoodComponent() {
  return (
    <>
      <td>Column 1</td>
      <td>Column 2</td>
    </>
  );
}

// NOTE: The shorthand <> does not support keys.
// If mapping over a list, you must use the full syntax:
const list = items.map(item => (
  <React.Fragment key={item.id}>
    <dt>{item.term}</dt>
    <dd>{item.desc}</dd>
  </React.Fragment>
));
Hinglish Explanation:
React me ek niyam hai ki aap hamesha ek hi main parent element return kar sakte ho. Pehle log aalsi bankar sab kuch ek faltu <div> ke andar daal dete the. Isse HTML tag ka kachra jama ho jata tha aur CSS Flexbox toot jata tha.
React Fragments (yani <></>) aisi invisible diwaren hain jo components ko ek group me baandhti hain taki React khush rahe, par jab HTML screen pe banta hai, toh wo invisible tags gayab ho jate hain aur DOM me koi faltu element nahi judta.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Fragments group a list of children without adding extra nodes to the DOM.
  • Essential for maintaining correct HTML structures (like returning <td> elements into a <tr>) and CSS Grid/Flexbox layouts.
  • Shorthand is <> </>.
  • If you need to assign a key prop in a mapped list, you must use the full <React.Fragment key={id}> syntax.
Q59

Explain hydration mismatch.

▼

Deep Dive: React Strict Mode

If you've ever used console.log in a React Component and wondered why it prints twice, you've experienced React Strict Mode.

<React.StrictMode> is a developer tool (wrapper component) that helps identify potential problems in an application. It does not render any visible UI. It strictly only runs in Development mode; it is completely stripped out and disabled in Production builds, so it does not affect live performance.

What does it actually do?

  1. Intentional Double-Rendering: It intentionally invokes your functional components and state updater functions twice in rapid succession. Why? Because React expects your render functions to be "Pure" (without side effects). If your component accidentally mutates external variables, the double-render will immediately expose the bug by showing broken, multiplied data on your screen.
  2. Identifies Unsafe Lifecycles: Warns you if you are using deprecated, buggy class lifecycle methods.
  3. Detects Legacy String Refs: Warns against using old string-based refs.

Real-World Analogy:

Strict Mode is like a harsh Drill Sergeant during army training. The Sergeant makes you do every exercise twice. If your form is bad, doing it twice guarantees you'll fail and get caught so you can fix it. But when it's time for actual combat (Production), the Sergeant goes away, and you only do the action once, perfectly.

jsx 
// In index.js
import { StrictMode } from 'react';
import { createRoot } from 'react-dom/client';

const root = createRoot(document.getElementById('root'));
root.render(
  // Wrapping the app enables the checks
  <StrictMode>
    <App />
  </StrictMode>
);

// In App.js
function App() {
  // If you see this log TWICE in the dev console, 
  // StrictMode is working as intended!
  console.log("App is rendering..."); 
  
  return <div>Hello World</div>;
}
Hinglish Explanation:
Naye developers hamesha pareshan hote hain ki unka console.log React me do baar kyun chal raha hai. Ye bug nahi hai, ye React Strict Mode ka feature hai!
Strict Mode development ke time pe aapke component ko jaan-bujh kar 2 baar chalata hai. Aisa isliye taki agar aapne apne code me galti se koi side-effect (kachra data) dala hoga jo component ko impure bana raha hai, toh 2 baar chalne ki wajah se wo galti screen pe turant samne aa jayegi. Production (Live website) me ye apne aap band ho jata hai aur code 1 hi baar chalta hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Strict mode purposely double-invokes components to detect impure functions and side-effect bugs.
  • It only runs in Development Mode. It has zero impact on Production.
  • It throws warnings for legacy, deprecated React APIs (like old string refs or unsafe lifecycles).
Q60

How would you optimize a large React app?

▼

Deep Dive: Lazy Loading & Suspense

When you build a React SPA, tools like Webpack bundle your entire application into one massive JavaScript file. If your app has 50 pages and an admin dashboard with heavy charts, a user visiting just the "Login" page still has to download all 5MB of code for the entire site before they can click anything. This destroys initial load performance.

React.lazy enables Code Splitting. It tells Webpack to split the heavy component into a completely separate JS file chunk. That specific chunk is only downloaded over the network when the user actually navigates to that page.

Suspense is the companion component. Because fetching the separate JS file takes time (network request), React needs to know what to show on the screen while it's downloading. <Suspense fallback={...}> provides a loading spinner during that gap.

Real-World Analogy:

Without Lazy Loading: Going to a restaurant and the waiter forces you to read the entire 50-page recipe book for every dish they serve before you are allowed to order a glass of water.

With Lazy Loading & Suspense: You sit down and order water instantly. When you decide you want steak, the waiter hands you just the 1-page steak menu. While he fetches that menu from the back, he gives you breadsticks (Suspense Fallback) so you aren't staring at an empty table.

jsx 
import React, { Suspense, lazy } from 'react';

// Normal Import (Bundled immediately into main file)
import Home from './Home';

// Lazy Import (Code-split into a separate chunk)
// This won't be downloaded until the user tries to view it!
const HeavyDashboard = lazy(() => import('./HeavyDashboard'));

function App() {
  return (
    <div>
      <Home />
      
      {/* Suspense catches the loading state of the lazy component */}
      <Suspense fallback={<div>Loading massive dashboard...</div>}>
         <HeavyDashboard />
      </Suspense>
    </div>
  );
}
Hinglish Explanation:
Normal React me app ki har file milkar ek bohot badi bundle file ban jati hai jise user ko shuruat me hi download karna padta hai, jisse loading bohot slow ho jati hai.
Lazy Loading se hum React ko bolte hain ki "Bhai, Admin Dashboard ka code tabhi download karna jab user admin button pe click kare". Isse shuruat ki loading bohot fast ho jati hai (Code Splitting).
Par jab click karne ke baad code internet se download ho raha hota hai, toh us gap me screen khali na rahe isliye hum us component ko Suspense ke andar daal dete hain, jo gap me ek loading spinner dikha deta hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Lazy Loading: Dynamically importing components to enable Code Splitting. Reduces the initial bundle size significantly.
  • Suspense: A wrapper component that provides a fallback UI (like a spinner) while the lazy-loaded chunk is being downloaded over the network.
  • Essential optimization technique for large-scale enterprise applications to achieve fast initial load times (TTI - Time to Interactive).
Q121

What are React Server Components (RSC) and how do they differ from Client Components?

▼

Deep Dive: React Server Components (RSC)

React Server Components are a paradigm shift introduced in React 18+ and fully embraced in Next.js 13+ (App Router). They allow components to execute entirely on the server, never shipping their JavaScript to the client browser.

Key differences:

  • Server Components (default in App Router): Run only on the server. Can directly access databases, file systems, and internal APIs. Their JS is never sent to the client bundle, resulting in zero bundle size impact.
  • Client Components ('use client'): Run on the client (and may pre-render on server). Required for interactivity — useState, useEffect, event handlers, browser APIs.

The Mental Model:

Server Components render on the server and stream their output (as a special RSC payload, not plain HTML) to the client. The client then stitches this payload into the component tree alongside Client Components. This is NOT the same as SSR — SSR produces HTML strings, while RSC produces a rich, serializable component tree.

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine a restaurant kitchen (server) and the dining table (client). Server Components are like dishes that are fully prepared in the kitchen and only the finished plate arrives at the table — the customer never sees the recipe or ingredients. Client Components are like a DIY salad bar — the raw ingredients (JavaScript) are sent to the table, and the customer assembles (renders) it themselves.

javascript 
// ✅ Server Component (default — no directive needed)
// This component's JS never reaches the browser
async function UserProfile({ userId }) {
  // Direct DB access — impossible in client components!
  const user = await db.query('SELECT * FROM users WHERE id = $1', [userId]);

  return (
    <div>
      <h1>{user.name}</h1>
      <p>{user.bio}</p>
      <LikeButton initialCount={user.likes} /> {/* Client Component */}
    </div>
  );
}

// ✅ Client Component — needs the directive
'use client';
import { useState } from 'react';

function LikeButton({ initialCount }) {
  const [likes, setLikes] = useState(initialCount);
  return <button onClick={() => setLikes(l => l + 1)}>❤️ {likes}</button>;
}
Hinglish Explanation:
React Server Components ek naya concept hai jisme kuch components sirf server pe chalte hain aur unka JavaScript client ko kabhi nahi bheja jata.
Socho ek restaurant hai — Server Component wo dish hai jo kitchen me poori ban kar sirf plate me aati hai (user ko recipe nahi milti). Client Component wo salad bar hai jahan ingredients (JS code) user ke browser pe aate hain aur wahan assemble hote hain.
Benefit: Jo components interactive nahi hain (data dikhana, DB se fetch karna), unke liye user ke browser pe unnecessary JavaScript nahi jaata, toh app bohot fast ho jaati hai.
'use client' directive lagana padta hai jab aapko useState, onClick, ya browser API use karni ho.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Server Components: Run on server, zero client JS, can access DB/filesystem directly, cannot use hooks or event handlers.
  • Client Components: Use 'use client' directive, required for interactivity (state, effects, events).
  • RSC ≠ SSR: SSR sends HTML strings; RSC sends a serializable component tree payload that integrates with the client React tree.
  • Server Components can import Client Components, but Client Components cannot import Server Components (they can accept them as children/props though).
Q122

When should you use useReducer instead of useState?

▼

Deep Dive: useReducer — The State Machine Hook

useReducer is an alternative to useState for managing complex state logic. It follows the Redux pattern: (state, action) → newState.

When to use useReducer over useState:

  • Multiple sub-values: When state is an object with many interdependent fields (e.g., a form with validation).
  • Complex transitions: When the next state depends on the previous state in non-trivial ways.
  • Action-driven updates: When you want named, predictable state transitions (like a state machine).
  • Testability: Reducer functions are pure — easy to unit test without rendering components.

Real-World Analogy:

useState is like a simple light switch — ON or OFF. useReducer is like a smart home control panel — one dashboard that handles lights, AC, music, and security based on named commands ("movie mode", "sleep mode", "party mode").

javascript 
// Complex form state — perfect useReducer use case
const initialState = { name: '', email: '', errors: {}, isSubmitting: false };

function formReducer(state, action) {
  switch (action.type) {
    case 'SET_FIELD':
      return { ...state, [action.field]: action.value, 
               errors: { ...state.errors, [action.field]: '' } };
    case 'SET_ERRORS':
      return { ...state, errors: action.errors, isSubmitting: false };
    case 'SUBMIT':
      return { ...state, isSubmitting: true };
    case 'RESET':
      return initialState;
    default:
      throw new Error(`Unknown action: ${action.type}`);
  }
}

function SignupForm() {
  const [state, dispatch] = useReducer(formReducer, initialState);

  const handleSubmit = () => {
    dispatch({ type: 'SUBMIT' });
    // validate and submit...
  };

  return (
    <input
      value={state.name}
      onChange={(e) => dispatch({ 
        type: 'SET_FIELD', field: 'name', value: e.target.value 
      })}
    />
  );
}
Hinglish Explanation:
useState ek simple switch hai — value set karo, ho gaya. Par jab aapka state bohot complex ho jaye (form ke 5 fields + validation + loading state), toh 5 alag-alag useState manage karna mushkil ho jata hai.
useReducer ek smart control panel hai — ek reducer function likhte ho jo ek central jagah pe sab state changes handle karta hai named actions ke through (jaise "SET_FIELD", "SUBMIT", "RESET").
Bonus: Reducer function pure function hai, toh unit test karna aasan hai — bina React component render kiye test kar sakte ho.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • useState: Simple, independent state values. Good for toggles, counters, single inputs.
  • useReducer: Complex state objects with interdependent updates. Good for forms, wizards, state machines.
  • Reducer functions are pure — given the same state + action, they always return the same new state.
  • Often combined with useContext to create a lightweight Redux-like global state without external libraries.
Q123

How do you design effective Custom Hooks?

▼

Deep Dive: Custom Hooks — Extracting Reusable Logic

Custom Hooks are JavaScript functions that start with use and can call other hooks. They let you extract stateful logic out of components so it can be shared across multiple components without changing hierarchy.

Design Principles:

  1. Single Responsibility: Each hook should do one thing well (e.g., useFetch, useLocalStorage, useDebounce).
  2. Return What's Needed: Return an object or tuple with state + actions the consumer needs.
  3. Accept Configuration: Use parameters for flexibility (URLs, delays, options).
  4. Handle Cleanup: Always clean up subscriptions, timers, and event listeners in the effect's return function.

Real-World Analogy:

Custom Hooks are like USB adapters — a standardized plug that provides a specific capability (data fetching, form validation, window resize tracking). Any device (component) can use the same adapter without knowing its internal wiring.

javascript 
// Custom Hook: useFetch — handles loading, error, data, and caching
function useFetch(url) {
  const [state, setState] = useState({ 
    data: null, loading: true, error: null 
  });

  useEffect(() => {
    const controller = new AbortController(); // cleanup on unmount!

    setState({ data: null, loading: true, error: null });
    
    fetch(url, { signal: controller.signal })
      .then(res => {
        if (!res.ok) throw new Error(`HTTP ${res.status}`);
        return res.json();
      })
      .then(data => setState({ data, loading: false, error: null }))
      .catch(err => {
        if (err.name !== 'AbortError') {
          setState({ data: null, loading: false, error: err.message });
        }
      });

    return () => controller.abort(); // Cleanup!
  }, [url]);

  return state; // { data, loading, error }
}

// Usage — clean and reusable across any component
function UserList() {
  const { data: users, loading, error } = useFetch('/api/users');
  if (loading) return <Spinner />;
  if (error) return <Error message={error} />;
  return users.map(u => <UserCard key={u.id} user={u} />);
}
Hinglish Explanation:
Custom Hooks ka matlab hai ki aap apni stateful logic (jo useState, useEffect use karti hai) ko ek alag function me nikal sakte ho aur use kisi bhi component me reuse kar sakte ho.
Jaise useFetch — ek baar likho, aur har jagah use karo jahan data fetch karna ho. Har component ko sirf { data, loading, error } milta hai, andar ka complexity chhupa rahta hai.
Rules: Function ka naam use se shuru hona chahiye, aur andar React hooks call ho sakte hain. Cleanup karna mat bhoolo (jaise AbortController se fetch cancel karna jab component unmount ho).

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Custom Hooks must start with use — this enables the Rules of Hooks linter.
  • They share stateful logic, not state itself — each call gets its own independent state.
  • Always handle cleanup (AbortController, clearTimeout, removeEventListener) to prevent memory leaks.
  • Popular patterns: useFetch, useLocalStorage, useDebounce, useMediaQuery, useOnClickOutside.
Q124

What is the difference between React.memo, useMemo, and useCallback?

▼

Deep Dive: The Performance Optimization Trifecta

These three tools solve different performance problems, but all revolve around one core concept: memoization — caching results to avoid redundant computation.

  • React.memo(Component): A Higher-Order Component that wraps a component. It performs a shallow comparison of props. If props haven't changed, it skips re-rendering the component entirely. Optimizes rendering.
  • useMemo(() => value, [deps]): A hook that memoizes a computed value. It only re-computes when dependencies change. Optimizes expensive calculations.
  • useCallback((args) => {}, [deps]): A hook that memoizes a function reference. It returns the same function instance across renders unless dependencies change. Optimizes reference stability for child components wrapped in React.memo.

Real-World Analogy:

React.memo is a bouncer at a club door — "Has your invitation (props) changed? No? Then I won't re-check you." useMemo is a calculator with memory — "I already solved 2+2, so I'll just recall 4." useCallback is a phone number on speed dial — "It's the same number as last time, so I won't create a new contact card."

javascript 
// React.memo — Prevents re-render if props don't change
const ExpensiveList = React.memo(function ExpensiveList({ items }) {
  return items.map(item => <div key={item.id}>{item.name}</div>);
});

function App() {
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0);
  const [items] = useState([{ id: 1, name: 'A' }, { id: 2, name: 'B' }]);

  // useMemo — Caches expensive computation
  const sortedItems = useMemo(() => {
    console.log('Sorting...'); // Only runs when items change
    return [...items].sort((a, b) => a.name.localeCompare(b.name));
  }, [items]);

  // useCallback — Stable function reference for memo'd children
  const handleClick = useCallback((id) => {
    console.log('Clicked:', id);
  }, []); // Same reference across re-renders

  return (
    <div>
      <button onClick={() => setCount(c => c + 1)}>Count: {count}</button>
      <ExpensiveList items={sortedItems} onClick={handleClick} />
    </div>
  );
  // When count changes: App re-renders, but ExpensiveList does NOT
  // because sortedItems and handleClick references are stable
}
Hinglish Explanation:
Teeno cheezein memoization (caching) ke baare me hain, par alag-alag cheezein cache karti hain:
React.memo: Component ko wrap karta hai — "Agar props nahi badle, toh re-render mat karo." Jaise bouncer bole "Tera invitation same hai? Ja andar bina check ke."
useMemo: Ek value ko cache karta hai — "Yeh heavy calculation pehle kar chuka hu, result yaad hai, dubara nahi karunga."
useCallback: Ek function reference ko cache karta hai — "Yeh wahi function hai jo pehle tha, naya nahi banauga." Yeh tab zaroori hai jab wo function React.memo wale child ko pass ho raha ho, warna naya reference banne se memo tod dega.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • React.memo: Memoizes a component — skips re-render if props are shallowly equal.
  • useMemo: Memoizes a computed value — avoids re-computation on every render.
  • useCallback: Memoizes a function reference — keeps the same identity unless deps change.
  • useCallback(fn, deps) is equivalent to useMemo(() => fn, deps).
  • Don't prematurely optimize — these add complexity. Profile first, then optimize proven bottlenecks.
Q125

What is React Fiber and how does it improve rendering?

▼

Deep Dive: React Fiber — The Internal Reconciliation Engine

React Fiber is the complete rewrite of React's core reconciliation algorithm (introduced in React 16). The old "Stack Reconciler" processed the component tree synchronously in one go — if the tree was huge, the main thread was blocked, causing UI jank.

What Fiber Changed:

  • Incremental Rendering: Fiber breaks rendering work into small units of work (fibers). Each fiber represents one component in the tree.
  • Interruptible Work: React can pause rendering, handle higher-priority updates (like user input), and resume later.
  • Priority-Based Scheduling: Updates are assigned priority lanes. Typing in an input (high priority) can interrupt a list re-render (low priority).
  • Two-Phase Rendering: (1) Render Phase (interruptible) — builds the fiber tree, diffs changes. (2) Commit Phase (synchronous) — applies actual DOM mutations.

Real-World Analogy:

The old Stack Reconciler was like a painter who must finish an entire wall before answering any questions. React Fiber is like a painter who paints in small patches — when the boss walks in with an urgent request (user typing), the painter can pause, handle the request, and resume painting.

javascript 
// Fiber enables Concurrent Features like useTransition
import { useState, useTransition } from 'react';

function SearchResults() {
  const [query, setQuery] = useState('');
  const [results, setResults] = useState([]);
  const [isPending, startTransition] = useTransition();

  const handleChange = (e) => {
    // HIGH PRIORITY — update input immediately
    setQuery(e.target.value);

    // LOW PRIORITY — Fiber can interrupt this if user keeps typing
    startTransition(() => {
      const filtered = hugeDataset.filter(item => 
        item.name.includes(e.target.value)
      );
      setResults(filtered);
    });
  };

  return (
    <div>
      <input value={query} onChange={handleChange} />
      {isPending && <span>Updating...</span>}
      {results.map(r => <div key={r.id}>{r.name}</div>)}
    </div>
  );
}
Hinglish Explanation:
Purana React (Stack Reconciler) ek painter tha jo poori wall ek saans me paint karta tha — beech me ruk nahi sakta tha. Agar wall bohot badi ho (heavy UI), toh tab tak user ka click ya typing respond nahi hota tha (UI freeze).
React Fiber ne rendering ko chhote-chhote tukdon (units of work) me tod diya. Ab React kuch component render kare, beech me agar user ne kuch type kiya (high priority), toh React rendering pause karke pehle typing handle kare, fir resume kare.
Iske wajah se useTransition aur Suspense jaise features possible hue. React ab concurrent hai — ek saath multiple cheezein handle kar sakta hai bina UI freeze kiye.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Fiber is React's internal reconciliation engine (since React 16), enabling incremental, interruptible rendering.
  • Work is split into small units (fibers), each representing one component node.
  • Enables Concurrent Features: useTransition, useDeferredValue, Suspense.
  • Two phases: Render Phase (interruptible, builds virtual tree) → Commit Phase (synchronous, applies DOM changes).
Q126

What are advanced use cases of useRef beyond DOM access?

▼

Deep Dive: useRef — The Mutable Box That Survives Re-renders

useRef returns a mutable object { current: value } that persists across re-renders without triggering re-renders when its value changes. While most developers know it for DOM element references, it has powerful advanced use cases:

  1. Storing Previous State Values: Track what a value was in the previous render.
  2. Storing Mutable Flags: Keep "instance variables" like isMounted, isFirstRender.
  3. Holding Timer/Interval IDs: Store setTimeout/setInterval IDs for cleanup.
  4. Avoiding Stale Closures: Keep a ref to the latest callback to avoid stale values in long-lived effects.

Real-World Analogy:

useRef is like a sticky note on your desk. Changing what's written on the note doesn't ring a bell (no re-render). But the note is always there for you to check. useState is like a doorbell — changing it rings the bell (triggers re-render).

javascript 
// 1. Track previous value
function usePrevious(value) {
  const ref = useRef();
  useEffect(() => { ref.current = value; });
  return ref.current; // returns value from PREVIOUS render
}

// 2. Avoid stale closures in intervals
function Timer() {
  const [count, setCount] = useState(0);
  const countRef = useRef(count);
  countRef.current = count; // Always points to latest count

  useEffect(() => {
    const id = setInterval(() => {
      // ✅ countRef.current is always fresh
      console.log('Current count:', countRef.current);
    }, 1000);
    return () => clearInterval(id);
  }, []); // Empty deps — but ref keeps it fresh!

  return <button onClick={() => setCount(c => c + 1)}>{count}</button>;
}

// 3. Store interval ID for cleanup
function Stopwatch() {
  const intervalRef = useRef(null);
  const [seconds, setSeconds] = useState(0);

  const start = () => {
    intervalRef.current = setInterval(() => 
      setSeconds(s => s + 1), 1000
    );
  };
  const stop = () => clearInterval(intervalRef.current);

  return <div>{seconds}s <button onClick={start}>Start</button></div>;
}
Hinglish Explanation:
useRef ek sticky note hai jo re-renders ke beech survive karta hai, par uski value change karne se component re-render nahi hota.
DOM access toh basic use hai, par advanced uses hain: (1) Previous value track karna, (2) Timer IDs store karna cleanup ke liye, (3) Stale closures se bachna — jab setInterval ke andar state ki purani value aati hai, toh ref me latest value rakh kar fresh value access kar sakte ho.
useState change = doorbell bajti hai (re-render). useRef change = sticky note update hoti hai, koi bell nahi bajti.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • useRef returns { current } — a mutable container that persists across renders.
  • Changing .current does NOT trigger a re-render (unlike useState).
  • Key patterns: previous value tracking, timer ID storage, stale closure prevention, instance-like variables.
  • Think of useRef as class component's this.instanceVar equivalent in functional components.
Q127

What are HOC, Render Props, and Hooks — and how did they evolve?

▼

Deep Dive: The Evolution of Code Reuse in React

React has gone through three major patterns for sharing logic between components:

  1. Higher-Order Components (HOC) — Era 1: A function that takes a component and returns a new enhanced component. Example: withAuth(Dashboard). Problem: "Wrapper Hell" — too many nested layers make debugging nightmarish, and prop collisions occur when multiple HOCs inject props with the same name.
  2. Render Props — Era 2: A component that takes a function as children or render prop, calling it with shared data. Problem: "Callback Hell" — deeply nested render functions reduce readability.
  3. Custom Hooks — Era 3 (Current): Extract logic into use* functions. No wrapper nesting, no callback hell, composable, and testable. This is the recommended approach.
javascript 
// 1. HOC Pattern — wraps component, injects props
function withWindowWidth(WrappedComponent) {
  return function Enhanced(props) {
    const [width, setWidth] = useState(window.innerWidth);
    useEffect(() => {
      const handle = () => setWidth(window.innerWidth);
      window.addEventListener('resize', handle);
      return () => window.removeEventListener('resize', handle);
    }, []);
    return <WrappedComponent {...props} windowWidth={width} />;
  };
}
const ResponsiveNav = withWindowWidth(Navbar); // Usage

// 2. Render Props Pattern — passes data via function child
function WindowWidth({ children }) {
  const [width, setWidth] = useState(window.innerWidth);
  // ... same effect as above
  return children(width); // call the function with data
}
// Usage: <WindowWidth>{(width) => <div>{width}px</div>}</WindowWidth>

// 3. Custom Hook — cleanest approach ✅
function useWindowWidth() {
  const [width, setWidth] = useState(window.innerWidth);
  useEffect(() => {
    const handle = () => setWidth(window.innerWidth);
    window.addEventListener('resize', handle);
    return () => window.removeEventListener('resize', handle);
  }, []);
  return width;
}
// Usage: const width = useWindowWidth();
Hinglish Explanation:
React me logic share karne ke teen yugs hain:
HOC (Purana): Ek function jo component le aur enhanced component de. Problem: Bahut saare wrappers lagne lagte — debugging me wrapper ke andar wrapper ke andar wrapper dikhta (Wrapper Hell).
Render Props (Beech wala): Component apne children ko function ki tarah call karta hai data ke saath. Problem: Bohot nesting ho jati hai (Callback Hell).
Custom Hooks (Naya ✅): Logic ko ek use* function me nikal lo. Koi wrapper nahi, koi nesting nahi, seedha const width = useWindowWidth() likh lo. Sabse clean aur recommended approach.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • HOC: Wraps components → Wrapper Hell, prop collisions. Still used in some libraries (e.g., Redux's connect()).
  • Render Props: Function-as-children pattern → Callback Hell. Still valid but less preferred.
  • Custom Hooks: The modern, recommended pattern — flat, composable, testable, no wrapper nesting.
  • All three solve the same problem: sharing stateful logic between components without duplicating code.
Q128

What are React Portals and when should you use them?

▼

Deep Dive: React Portals — Escaping the DOM Hierarchy

React Portals provide a way to render children into a different DOM node that exists outside the parent component's DOM hierarchy, while still maintaining the same React component tree (events still bubble up through the React tree, not the DOM tree).

Use Cases:

  • Modals/Dialogs: Render outside the parent's overflow: hidden or z-index stacking context.
  • Tooltips/Popovers: Position elements relative to the viewport, not a clipped parent.
  • Toast Notifications: Render at the document body level to overlay everything.

Key Insight: Even though the modal is rendered in a completely different DOM node (document.getElementById('modal-root')), React events (like onClick) still bubble up through the React component tree, not the actual DOM tree. This means a click inside a portalled modal will still trigger a parent component's event handler.

javascript 
import { createPortal } from 'react-dom';

function Modal({ children, isOpen, onClose }) {
  if (!isOpen) return null;

  // Renders into #modal-root (outside #root in index.html)
  return createPortal(
    <div className="modal-overlay" onClick={onClose}>
      <div className="modal-content" onClick={(e) => e.stopPropagation()}>
        <button className="close-btn" onClick={onClose}>✕</button>
        {children}
      </div>
    </div>,
    document.getElementById('modal-root') // Target DOM node
  );
}

// index.html needs: <div id="modal-root"></div> alongside <div id="root">
// Usage:
function App() {
  const [showModal, setShowModal] = useState(false);
  return (
    <div style={{ overflow: 'hidden' }}> {/* overflow won't clip modal! */}
      <button onClick={() => setShowModal(true)}>Open</button>
      <Modal isOpen={showModal} onClose={() => setShowModal(false)}>
        <h2>This renders outside the parent DOM!</h2>
      </Modal>
    </div>
  );
}
Hinglish Explanation:
React me normally sab components apne parent ke andar DOM me render hote hain. Par kabhi-kabhi aapko parent ke DOM se bahar render karna padta hai — jaise Modal jo poori screen pe aaye, tooltip jo clip na ho.
createPortal se aap component ko kisi bhi DOM node me render kar sakte ho (jaise #modal-root), par React ke liye wo abhi bhi apne parent component ka child hai — events, context, sab normally kaam karte hain.
Sochiye: Aap apne ghar (parent div) me khade ho par ek secret tunnel se doosri building me dikh rahe ho. Ghar ke rules (React tree) abhi bhi laagu hain, par physically aap doosri jagah ho (DOM tree).

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Portals render children into a different DOM node while keeping the same React component tree.
  • Events bubble through the React tree, not the DOM tree — portalled content behaves like a normal child in React.
  • Use for: modals, tooltips, toasts — anything that needs to escape overflow: hidden or z-index constraints.
  • Syntax: createPortal(children, domNode) from react-dom.
Q129

What is the Compound Component Pattern in React?

▼

Deep Dive: Compound Component Pattern

The Compound Component Pattern is an advanced React design pattern where a set of components work together to share an implicit state. The parent manages the state, and children communicate with the parent via Context — without the consumer needing to wire up props manually.

Think of HTML's native <select> and <option>:

You don't pass an onChange to each <option>. The <select> manages the state, and <option> children just declare their values. Compound Components replicate this pattern in React.

Benefits:

  • Flexible API: Consumer controls the layout and composition — no rigid prop configurations.
  • Implicit State Sharing: No manual prop drilling between parent and children.
  • Used by: Libraries like Headless UI, Radix UI, Reach UI, and Chakra UI.
javascript 
// Compound Component: Accordion
const AccordionContext = createContext();

function Accordion({ children, defaultIndex = 0 }) {
  const [activeIndex, setActiveIndex] = useState(defaultIndex);
  return (
    <AccordionContext.Provider value={{ activeIndex, setActiveIndex }}>
      <div className="accordion">{children}</div>
    </AccordionContext.Provider>
  );
}

function AccordionItem({ index, children }) {
  const { activeIndex, setActiveIndex } = useContext(AccordionContext);
  const isOpen = activeIndex === index;
  return (
    <div className="accordion-item">
      <button onClick={() => setActiveIndex(isOpen ? -1 : index)}>
        {isOpen ? '▲' : '▼'}
      </button>
      {isOpen && <div className="content">{children}</div>}
    </div>
  );
}

// Attach sub-components to parent
Accordion.Item = AccordionItem;

// Usage — Clean, flexible, declarative API
<Accordion defaultIndex={0}>
  <Accordion.Item index={0}>Section 1 content</Accordion.Item>
  <Accordion.Item index={1}>Section 2 content</Accordion.Item>
  <Accordion.Item index={2}>Section 3 content</Accordion.Item>
</Accordion>
Hinglish Explanation:
Compound Component Pattern HTML ke <select> + <option> jaisa hai — parent (select) state manage karta hai, children (options) sirf apni value declare karte hain. Beech me koi manual prop passing nahi.
React me isko Context se implement karte hain — parent ek Provider banata hai shared state ke saath, aur children useContext se us state ko access karte hain.
Real-world example: Accordion, Tabs, Dropdown menu — jahan ek parent ko pata hona chahiye ki kaun sa child active hai, par consumer ko sirf declarative syntax chahiye bina manual wiring ke.
Libraries jaise Headless UI, Radix, Chakra — sab yahi pattern use karti hain.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Compound Components share implicit state via Context between parent and children.
  • Inspired by native HTML elements like <select> + <option>, <table> + <tr> + <td>.
  • Provides a flexible, declarative API — consumer controls composition, parent manages logic.
  • Used extensively in UI libraries (Headless UI, Radix, Chakra) for Accordions, Tabs, Dropdowns.
Q130

How should you approach testing React components?

▼

Deep Dive: React Testing Strategy — The Testing Trophy

Kent C. Dodds' Testing Trophy (an evolution of the Testing Pyramid) recommends the following priority for React apps:

  1. Integration Tests (Most Valuable): Test how multiple components work together. Render a feature, simulate user interactions, and verify outcomes. Use React Testing Library.
  2. Unit Tests: Test pure functions, utility helpers, reducers, and custom hooks in isolation. Use Jest / Vitest.
  3. End-to-End Tests: Test full user flows through the real browser. Use Playwright / Cypress.
  4. Static Analysis: TypeScript + ESLint catch bugs at compile time — the cheapest form of testing.

Key Principle: "Test the way your user uses the app, not the implementation details." Don't test internal state values — test what the user sees and does.

javascript 
// Integration Test with React Testing Library
import { render, screen, fireEvent, waitFor } from '@testing-library/react';
import userEvent from '@testing-library/user-event';
import LoginForm from './LoginForm';

test('shows error on invalid email and submits on valid input', async () => {
  const mockSubmit = jest.fn();
  render(<LoginForm onSubmit={mockSubmit} />);

  // Simulate user behavior — not implementation details!
  const emailInput = screen.getByLabelText(/email/i);
  const submitBtn = screen.getByRole('button', { name: /submit/i });

  // Test validation error
  await userEvent.type(emailInput, 'invalid');
  await userEvent.click(submitBtn);
  expect(screen.getByText(/valid email/i)).toBeInTheDocument();
  expect(mockSubmit).not.toHaveBeenCalled();

  // Test successful submission
  await userEvent.clear(emailInput);
  await userEvent.type(emailInput, 'user@test.com');
  await userEvent.type(screen.getByLabelText(/password/i), 'pass123');
  await userEvent.click(submitBtn);

  await waitFor(() => {
    expect(mockSubmit).toHaveBeenCalledWith({
      email: 'user@test.com', password: 'pass123'
    });
  });
});

// Unit Test for a pure reducer
test('formReducer handles SET_FIELD action', () => {
  const state = { name: '', email: '' };
  const result = formReducer(state, { 
    type: 'SET_FIELD', field: 'name', value: 'John' 
  });
  expect(result.name).toBe('John');
});
Hinglish Explanation:
React testing me sabse important rule hai: "User jaisa test karo, internal details mat test karo."
Matlab — state ki value check mat karo. User kya dekhta hai aur kya karta hai wo test karo. Jaise: "Email field me 'invalid' type karo, submit button click karo — error message dikhna chahiye."
Testing Trophy: Sabse zyada Integration Tests likho (RTL se — components milke kaise kaam karte hain), fir pure functions ke Unit Tests (reducer, helpers), aur kuch E2E Tests (Playwright se real browser me full flow).
Queries use karo: getByRole, getByLabelText, getByText — ye accessible hain aur user-centric testing promote karte hain.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Follow the Testing Trophy: Integration Tests > Unit Tests > E2E Tests > Static Analysis.
  • Use React Testing Library — test user behavior, not implementation details.
  • Prefer accessible queries: getByRole > getByLabelText > getByText > getByTestId.
  • Unit test pure functions (reducers, utils), integration test component interactions.
  • Mock external dependencies (API calls), not internal component logic.
🟢

Backend: Node & Express

libuv event loop, asynchronous non-blocking design, JWT security architectures, CORS configurations, rate-limit systems.
Q61

What is Node.js and why is it fast?

▼

Deep Dive: Node.js Architecture (V8 + libuv)

Node.js is not a language; it is a C++ runtime environment that allows JavaScript to run outside of the browser (on servers). Its incredible speed and ability to handle thousands of concurrent connections on a single thread come from its two core dependencies: V8 and libuv.

  • V8 Engine: The exact same JavaScript engine used in Google Chrome. Written in C++, it takes your raw JavaScript code and compiles it directly into lightning-fast machine code.
  • libuv: A multi-platform C library focused on asynchronous I/O. Because JS is single-threaded, it cannot naturally handle background tasks like reading files from a hard drive or querying a database without freezing. libuv provides the Event Loop and a hidden Thread Pool (typically 4 threads by default) to handle heavy OS-level tasks in the background, allowing the main V8 thread to keep accepting new incoming HTTP requests.

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine Node.js is a busy Restaurant.

V8: The Hostess at the front desk. She speaks the customer's language (JavaScript), takes their orders rapidly, and never stops moving. (The Single Thread).

libuv: The Kitchen Staff in the back. When the Hostess takes a complex order (read a huge file from disk), she hands the ticket to the Kitchen Staff (Thread Pool). The staff cooks it in the background while the Hostess immediately goes back to taking more orders. When the food is ready, the Kitchen rings a bell (Event Loop callback) and the Hostess delivers the food.

Hinglish Explanation:
Node.js browser ke bahar JS run karne ka ek tool hai. Ye do main cheezon se bana hai: V8 aur libuv.
V8 wahi engine hai jo Chrome me hota hai, ye aapke JS code ko compile karta hai. Par JS toh single-threaded hai! Agar koi file read karni ho (jisme 5 second lagenge), toh server baaki users ke liye hang ho jayega. Is problem ko libuv solve karta hai. libuv ek C++ library hai jiske paas apna ek secret "Thread Pool" (workers) hota hai. Jab bhi koi bhari I/O task aata hai (DB query, File read), V8 usko libuv ko de deta hai background me karne ke liye, aur khud dusre users ki request lene lagta hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Node.js = V8 (JS Engine) + libuv (Asynchronous C++ Library).
  • Node handles concurrency using an Event-Driven, Non-Blocking I/O model.
  • While the JS execution is single-threaded, the I/O operations (file system, network, cryptography) are handled by libuv's multi-threaded worker pool.
Q62

Difference between Node and browser JS runtime.

▼

Deep Dive: Event Loop in Node vs Browser

While both environments use an Event Loop to handle asynchronous operations, the Node.js event loop is significantly more complex because it has to manage operating system operations (File System, Networks) rather than just DOM events.

The Browser Event Loop: Mostly handles user clicks, HTTP requests, and timers. It simply relies on a Macrotask Queue and a Microtask Queue.

The Node.js Event Loop (libuv): Operates in distinct Phases. Each phase has its own FIFO queue of callbacks to execute:

  1. Timers: Executes setTimeout() and setInterval() callbacks.
  2. Pending Callbacks: Executes I/O callbacks deferred to the next loop iteration (e.g., TCP connection errors).
  3. Idle, Prepare: Internal use only.
  4. Poll: The most important phase. Retrieves new I/O events (e.g., file reading finished, incoming HTTP request). If the poll queue is empty, Node will block and wait here for new events!
  5. Check: Executes setImmediate() callbacks.
  6. Close Callbacks: Executes close events, like socket.on('close', ...).

Note on Microtasks: In Node, Microtasks (Promises and process.nextTick) have absolute highest priority. They are executed between every single phase of the event loop.

javascript 
const fs = require('fs');

console.log("1. Start");

// Phase 1: Timers
setTimeout(() => console.log("5. Timeout"), 0);

// Phase 5: Check
setImmediate(() => console.log("6. Immediate"));

// Microtasks (Run BEFORE the next phase starts)
process.nextTick(() => console.log("3. NextTick (Highest Priority!)"));
Promise.resolve().then(() => console.log("4. Promise"));

console.log("2. End");

// Output Order:
// 1. Start
// 2. End
// 3. NextTick
// 4. Promise
// 5. Timeout
// 6. Immediate
Hinglish Explanation:
Browser ka event loop simple hota hai, par Node.js ka event loop 6 alag-alag "Phases" (stages) me ghoomta hai. Har phase ki apni ek queue hoti hai.
Sabse pehle Timers (setTimeout) chalte hain, fir I/O (files/network) ke callbacks (Poll phase me), aur aakhiri me setImmediate chalte hain. Ek sabse zaroori baat: process.nextTick aur Promises (Microtasks) kisi phase ka wait nahi karte, wo do phases ke beech me VIP entry maar ke sabse pehle chal jate hain!

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Node's event loop (via libuv) operates in strict Phases (Timers ➔ I/O Polling ➔ Check/setImmediate ➔ Close).
  • process.nextTick() has the highest asynchronous priority in Node. It runs immediately after the current operation completes, before the Event Loop even continues.
  • setImmediate() is designed to execute a script right after the current Poll phase completes (in the Check phase).
Q63

What is libuv?

▼

Deep Dive: CommonJS (CJS) vs ES Modules (ESM)

For a long time, JavaScript had no built-in module system (no way to easily split code across multiple files). Node.js created its own solution called CommonJS. Years later, the official ECMAScript standard released a native solution called ES Modules.

  • CommonJS (require / module.exports): The original Node.js module system. It is Synchronous and evaluated at Runtime. Because it runs synchronously, you can place a require() statement dynamically inside an if statement.
  • ES Modules (import / export): The modern, official standard used in both Browsers and Node.js. It is Asynchronous and statically analyzed at Compile-time. Because it is static, you cannot put an import inside an if statement (unless using dynamic import()), which is exactly what allows bundlers to perform Tree Shaking.

Real-World Analogy:

CommonJS: Going to a restaurant and ordering food as you eat. You eat the appetizer, and then depending on how full you are (an if statement), you ask the waiter to bring the main course (dynamic require). You have to wait synchronously for the food to arrive.

ES Modules: Ordering a fixed-menu meal prep service a week in advance. The company reads your exact order (Static Analysis), packs everything efficiently, removes things you didn't order (Tree Shaking), and delivers it all at once.

javascript 
// --- CommonJS (Node.js Default Legacy) ---
const fs = require('fs');

function loadConfig(isDev) {
    if (isDev) {
        // ✅ Valid in CJS: Dynamic, synchronous loading at runtime
        const devTools = require('./devTools'); 
        devTools.init();
    }
}
module.exports = { loadConfig };


// --- ES Modules (Modern Standard) ---
import fs from 'fs';
// ❌ INVALID: Static imports must be at the top level!
// if (isDev) { import devTools from './devTools'; }

// ✅ Valid ESM Export
export function loadConfig() { /* ... */ }

// Note: To use ESM in Node.js, you must set "type": "module" in package.json,
// or save the file with a .mjs extension.
Hinglish Explanation:
Jab Node.js bana tha tab JS me import karne ka koi official tareeka nahi tha, toh unhone CommonJS (require) banaya. Ye synchronous hota hai aur code run hote time variables import karta hai.
Baad me official JS community ne ES Modules (import/export) banaya. Ye modern hai aur static hota hai. Static matlab code run hone se pehle hi engine ko pata chal jata hai ki aapne kya kya import kiya hai. Isi static nature ki wajah se Webpack jaise tools unused code ko delete (Tree Shake) kar paate hain, jo require me possible nahi tha.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • CommonJS uses require() and is synchronous/dynamic.
  • ES Modules use import/export and are asynchronous/static.
  • ES Modules are the official standard and are required for bundler optimizations like Tree Shaking.
  • Node.js supports both, but defaults to CommonJS unless specified in package.json.
Q64

How does Node handle concurrency?

▼

Deep Dive: Express Middleware

Express.js is a minimalist web framework for Node.js. Its entire architecture is based on the concept of Middleware. A middleware is simply a JavaScript function that has access to the Request object (req), the Response object (res), and the next function in the application's request-response cycle.

When an HTTP request hits an Express server, it passes through a pipeline of middleware functions. Each middleware can:

  1. Execute any code (e.g., logging).
  2. Make changes to the request or response objects (e.g., parsing JSON bodies or adding a user ID).
  3. End the request-response cycle (e.g., sending a 401 Unauthorized response).
  4. Call next() to pass control to the next middleware in the pipeline.

Critical Rule: If a middleware does not end the request (using res.send()), it must call next(), otherwise the request will hang forever.

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine a factory assembly line checking bottles of water (the Request).
Middleware 1 (Security): Checks if the bottle has a cap. If no cap, throws it in the trash (Ends request, sends 400 Error). If it has a cap, passes it to the next station (calls next()).
Middleware 2 (Parser): Prints a barcode label on the bottle (Modifies the req object), then passes it on (calls next()).
Controller (Final Route): Packs the bottle into a box and ships it to the customer (Sends the final res.json()).

javascript 
const express = require('express');
const app = express();

// 1. Global Middleware (Runs on EVERY request)
// Built-in middleware to parse JSON bodies
app.use(express.json()); 

// Custom Logger Middleware
app.use((req, res, next) => {
    console.log(`[${new Date().toISOString()}] ${req.method} ${req.url}`);
    next(); // MUST call next() to continue!
});

// 2. Route-Specific Middleware (Auth Guard)
const checkAuth = (req, res, next) => {
    if (req.headers.authorization === 'secret-token') {
        req.user = { id: 1, role: 'admin' }; // Modifying the request object
        next(); // User is valid, proceed to route
    } else {
        res.status(401).json({ error: 'Unauthorized!' }); // Ends the request early!
    }
};

// 3. The Final Route Controller
// Notice how we inject the 'checkAuth' middleware before the final function
app.get('/api/dashboard', checkAuth, (req, res) => {
    // We have access to the modified req.user here!
    res.json({ message: `Welcome Admin ${req.user.id}` });
});
Hinglish Explanation:
Express.js me jab bhi koi request aati hai, toh wo seedha final function tak nahi jati. Wo ek pipeline se guzarti hai jise Middleware kehte hain.
Middleware functions beech me khade security guards ya assistants ki tarah hote hain. Ye check kar sakte hain ki user logged in hai ya nahi. Agar logged in hai, toh wo next() call kar dete hain taki request aage chali jaye. Agar nahi hai, toh wo wahi se error res.send() kar dete hain aur request aage nahi jati. Dhyan rahe, agar middleware me na aapne response bheja aur na hi next() call kiya, toh user ki screen load hi hoti reh jayegi (hang ho jayegi).

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Middleware are functions in the request-response pipeline.
  • They take three arguments: (req, res, next). (Error handling middleware takes four: err, req, res, next).
  • They can modify the request, end the response, or pass control using next().
  • Failing to call next() or res.send() results in a hanging request.
Q65

Explain middleware in Express.

▼

Deep Dive: RESTful API Principles

REST (Representational State Transfer) is an architectural style for designing networked applications. When an API is "RESTful", it means it adheres to a specific set of constraints and conventions, making it predictable and easy for frontends to consume.

The Core Principles of REST:

  1. Client-Server Separation: The frontend UI and backend data storage are completely independent.
  2. Statelessness: Every HTTP request must contain all the information needed for the server to understand it. The server does not store any session state about the client on the server side between requests (which makes scaling massive servers easy).
  3. Resource-Based URLs: APIs should be structured around "Resources" (Nouns) rather than actions (Verbs). The action is determined by the HTTP Method (GET, POST, PUT, DELETE).
text 
❌ BAD (RPC Style - Action in URL)
GET /getAllUsers
POST /createNewUser
POST /updateUser?id=5
POST /deleteUser?id=5

✅ GOOD (RESTful Style - Nouns in URL, HTTP Method decides action)
GET    /users       (Fetch list of users)
POST   /users       (Create a new user)
GET    /users/5     (Fetch specific user with ID 5)
PUT    /users/5     (Completely replace user 5)
PATCH  /users/5     (Partially update user 5)
DELETE /users/5     (Delete user 5)

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine a Library.
RPC (Bad): You tell the librarian: "Execute the action of giving me book 5." "Execute the action of burning book 5."
REST (Good): You point to the "Books" section (Resource). You hold up a sign saying "GET 5" to borrow it. You hold up a sign saying "DELETE 5" to remove it. The section is always just called "Books", the sign (HTTP Method) tells the librarian what to do.

Hinglish Explanation:
RESTful API banate waqt sabse bada rule ye hai ki URLs me hamesha "Nouns" (naam) hone chahiye, "Verbs" (kaam) nahi.
Bura tareeka: /getUserData ya /deleteUser.
Sahi tareeka: Hamesha URL /users rakho. Agar user lana hai toh GET request bhejo. Naya user banana hai toh usi URL pe POST bhejo. Delete karna hai toh DELETE method use karo. Dusra bada niyam Statelessness hai, matlab server ye yaad nahi rakhta ki pichli request kisne bheji thi, har nayi request me token bhej ke identity prove karni padti hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • REST uses nouns for URIs (/articles), not verbs (/getArticles).
  • It relies on standard HTTP Methods (GET = Read, POST = Create, PUT/PATCH = Update, DELETE = Delete).
  • Statelessness is critical: No client context is stored on the server between requests. All auth tokens must be sent with every request.
Q66

Difference between app.use() and route handlers.

▼

Deep Dive: HTTP Status Codes

When a server responds to an API request, it must include an HTTP Status Code. This 3-digit number is the universal language of the web, instantly telling the client (the browser or frontend app) exactly what happened to their request without them needing to parse the JSON body.

The 5 Classes of Status Codes:

  • 1xx (Informational): The request was received, continuing process. (Rarely handled manually).
  • 2xx (Success): 200 OK (Standard success), 201 Created (Successfully created a resource via POST), 204 No Content (Success, but no data to return, often used for DELETE).
  • 3xx (Redirection): 301 Moved Permanently, 304 Not Modified (Tells the browser to use its cached version).
  • 4xx (Client Error - Frontend's Fault): 400 Bad Request (Invalid JSON sent), 401 Unauthorized (Missing/Invalid Token), 403 Forbidden (Valid token, but you lack admin permissions), 404 Not Found.
  • 5xx (Server Error - Backend's Fault): 500 Internal Server Error (The backend crashed), 502 Bad Gateway, 503 Service Unavailable.

Real-World Analogy:

You go to a post office to send a package.

200s: The clerk takes it and says "Perfect, sent!"

300s: The clerk says "This department moved to building B, go there."

400s: The clerk says "You forgot to write the zip code, fix YOUR mistake and come back."

500s: The clerk's computer catches on fire and they scream "WE ARE CLOSED!"

Hinglish Explanation:
Backend jab bhi frontend ko data wapas bhejta hai, toh ek 3-digit number bhejta hai jise Status Code kehte hain. Ye frontend developers ke liye bohot zaroori hai.
Agar series 200 ki hai, matlab sab badhiya hai (Success).
Agar series 400 ki hai, matlab frontend ne koi galti ki hai. Jaise 400 (galat data bheja), 401 (login token nahi bheja), 403 (login toh hai, par admin permission nahi hai).
Agar series 500 ki hai, matlab backend ka server crash ho gaya hai (database fail, etc), isme frontend ki koi galti nahi hoti.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • 401 Unauthorized: Authentication failed (Who are you?).
  • 403 Forbidden: Authorization failed (I know who you are, but you aren't allowed here).
  • Returning proper status codes (instead of returning 200 OK with an error message inside the JSON) is mandatory for a true RESTful API.
Q67

What is REST and what makes an API RESTful?

▼

Deep Dive: GraphQL vs REST

REST has been the industry standard for decades, but it has two major scaling problems: Over-fetching and Under-fetching. Facebook created GraphQL to solve these exact issues.

  • Over-fetching (REST Problem): If you hit /api/users/1 to get a user's name, the REST API might return their name, email, address, 50 previous orders, and credit card info. You only wanted the name, but you wasted bandwidth downloading 5MB of useless data.
  • Under-fetching (REST Problem): If you want a user's name AND their latest post title, you might have to make two separate HTTP requests: /api/users/1 and then /api/users/1/posts. This is slow.

The GraphQL Solution:

GraphQL exposes only ONE single endpoint (e.g., /graphql). Instead of hitting different URLs, the frontend sends a specific "Query String" detailing exactly what data it wants. The backend parses this query and returns exactly what was asked for—nothing more, nothing less, in a single network request.

Real-World Analogy:

REST (Fixed Menu Buffet): You go to a restaurant and order "Meal #1". The waiter brings you a burger, fries, a salad, and a huge soda. You only wanted the burger, so you throw the rest away (Over-fetching).

GraphQL (Custom Chef): You go to a restaurant and tell the chef: "I want exactly 1 burger patty, 2 pickles, and a half glass of water." The chef gives you exactly that, nothing is wasted, and you got it all in one trip.

graphql 
# The Frontend sends this exact structure in the POST body to /graphql
query {
  user(id: "1") {
    name
    email
    latestPost {
      title
    }
  }
}

# The Backend responds with the EXACT same shape! (No overfetching)
{
  "data": {
    "user": {
      "name": "Alice",
      "email": "alice@gmail.com",
      "latestPost": {
        "title": "GraphQL is awesome!"
      }
    }
  }
}
Hinglish Explanation:
REST API me problem ye hoti hai ki backend engineer ne jo fix kar diya, wahi aayega. Agar aapko ek user ka sirf naam chahiye, par backend /users/1 pe uski poori history bhej raha hai, toh apka internet data waste hoga (Over-fetching).
Facebook ne GraphQL banaya. Isme frontend bata sakta hai ki usko specifically kya chahiye. Frontend JSON jaisi ek query bhejta hai: "Mujhe bas user ka name aur email do". Backend GraphQL engine us query ko padhta hai, aur exactly wahi 2 cheezein bhejta hai. Data bilkul waste nahi hota aur app bahut fast chalta hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • GraphQL operates on a single endpoint (usually via POST).
  • Solves Over-fetching (getting too much data) and Under-fetching (having to make multiple API requests).
  • Frontend queries precisely what it needs. The backend resolves the data dynamically.
  • It is strongly typed using a Schema Definition Language (SDL).
Q68

Difference between PUT and PATCH.

▼

Deep Dive: Authentication vs Authorization

These two terms are frequently confused, but they represent two completely different steps in the security pipeline.

  • Authentication (AuthN): Verifying who a user is. It answers the question: "Are you really the person you claim to be?" (e.g., logging in with a username/password, FaceID, or checking a JWT token). If this fails, the server returns 401 Unauthorized.
  • Authorization (AuthZ): Verifying what a user is allowed to do. It answers the question: "Do you have permission to access this specific resource?" (e.g., checking if the logged-in user has the 'admin' role to delete a post). If this fails, the server returns 403 Forbidden.

Real-World Analogy:

Imagine going to a restricted VIP Nightclub.

Authentication: The bouncer at the front door checks your ID card to prove your name and age. You are who you say you are. You are allowed inside the club.

Authorization: Inside the club, you try to walk into the VIP Champagne Room. A second bouncer stops you and checks if your wristband says "VIP". Your identity is proven (you are inside), but you lack the permissions for this specific room. You are kicked out of the VIP area (403 Forbidden).

javascript 
// 1. Authentication Middleware (Who are you?)
const authenticate = (req, res, next) => {
    const token = req.headers.authorization;
    if (!token) return res.status(401).send("Who are you? Please login.");
    
    const user = verifyToken(token); // e.g. { id: 5, role: 'guest' }
    req.user = user; // Identity proven! Attach to request.
    next();
};

// 2. Authorization Middleware (What can you do?)
const authorizeAdmin = (req, res, next) => {
    // We already know WHO they are (from auth step)
    // Now we check WHAT they can do
    if (req.user.role !== 'admin') {
        return res.status(403).send("You are not allowed to do this.");
    }
    next();
};

// 3. The Route
// Must pass both gates to reach the controller!
app.delete('/api/users/5', authenticate, authorizeAdmin, (req, res) => {
    res.send("User deleted!");
});
Hinglish Explanation:
Interview me ye farq aana bohot zaroori hai.
Authentication ka matlab hai pehchaan karna. "Aap kaun ho?" Jab aap password daalte ho aur server verify karta hai, toh wo Authentication hai. Fail hone pe 401 Error aata hai.
Authorization ka matlab hai permission check karna. "Aap kya kya kar sakte ho?" Aap logged in toh ho, par kya aapke paas Admin rights hain file delete karne ke? Agar nahi, toh Authorization fail ho jayega aur 403 Error aayega.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • AuthN (Authentication): Identity Verification (401 Unauthorized).
  • AuthZ (Authorization): Permission Verification (403 Forbidden).
  • Authentication must always happen before Authorization.
Q69

How does authentication differ from authorization?

▼

Deep Dive: JWT (JSON Web Tokens)

A JWT is an open standard used to securely transmit information between a client and a server as a JSON object. In modern web apps, it is the primary method for handling Stateless Authentication.

The Problem with traditional Sessions: Historically, when you logged in, the server created a session in its memory/database and sent you a Cookie with a Session ID. If the server restarted, or if you had millions of users, storing all those sessions in the backend database became extremely expensive and hard to scale across multiple servers.

The JWT Solution: A JWT shifts the storage burden to the client. When you log in, the server creates a JSON object with your info (e.g., { userId: 5, role: 'admin' }), mathematically signs it with a cryptographic Secret Key, and gives it to you. The server does NOT save this token in a database. When you make your next request, you send the JWT back. The server mathematically verifies the signature using its Secret Key. If the signature matches, the server trusts the JSON payload is authentic.

Real-World Analogy:

Session Auth: The bouncer has a 100-page guest list. Every time you enter a room, he spends 5 minutes searching the book for your name (Database lookup).

JWT Auth: The bouncer checks your ID once, gives you an official wax-stamped VIP wristband, and throws away his book. Now, whenever you enter a room, he just looks at the wax stamp on your wristband. No book needed! (Stateless).

Hinglish Explanation:
Pehle backend user ka data apne database me "Session" ke naam se save karta tha. Jab bohot users aa jate the toh database slow ho jata tha.
JWT (JSON Web Token) iska ilaj hai. Jab user login karta hai, server ek JSON banata hai, us par apna ek secret digital signature (stamp) lagata hai, aur token user ko de deta hai. Server apne paas kuch save nahi karta (Stateless)! Agli baar jab user request bhejta hai, server bas us token ka signature check karta hai. Agar signature original hai, matlab data sahi hai aur hacker ne chhed-chhad nahi ki hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • JWTs consist of three parts separated by dots: Header.Payload.Signature.
  • The Payload is Base64 encoded, NOT encrypted. Anyone can decode and read the data! Never put passwords in a JWT.
  • The Signature is what makes it secure. If a hacker alters the payload, the signature becomes invalid, and the server rejects it.
  • JWTs enable Stateless Authentication, making servers extremely easy to scale.
Q70

Explain JWT flow end-to-end.

▼

Deep Dive: OAuth 2.0 Flow

If you've ever used a "Log in with Google" or "Log in with GitHub" button, you have used OAuth 2.0. It is an industry-standard Delegated Authorization framework. It allows an application to access a user's data on another service without ever seeing the user's password.

The Standard Authorization Code Flow:

  1. Client Request: Your App redirects the user to Google's Authorization Server.
  2. User Consent: The user sees a Google prompt: "Your App wants access to your email. Allow?". The user clicks Yes.
  3. Auth Code: Google redirects the user back to Your App with a temporary Authorization Code in the URL.
  4. Token Exchange: Your App's Backend takes that Code, adds a secret key, and sends it directly (server-to-server) to Google.
  5. Access Token: Google verifies the secret and returns an Access Token.
  6. Resource Fetch: Your backend uses the Access Token to fetch the user's email from Google's API, and finally logs the user into your system.

Real-World Analogy:

You stay at a Hotel. You order a Pizza, but the delivery guy isn't allowed upstairs. You (User) call the Front Desk (Google) and say, "I authorize the Pizza Guy (Your App) to come to my room." The Front Desk gives the Pizza Guy a temporary Keycard (Access Token) that only works for Room 304, and expires in 1 hour. The Pizza Guy never needed to know your actual room password or master key.

Hinglish Explanation:
Agar aap apni app me "Login with Google" lagana chahte ho, toh OAuth 2.0 flow use hota hai.
Sabse pehle app user ko Google ki website pe bhejta hai. Waha user apna ID/Password daalta hai (Aapki app password kabhi nahi dekhti). Google user se poochta hai ki "Kya is app ko apka email padhne du?". Jab user Yes karta hai, toh Google usko waapas aapki app pe bhejta hai ek chote se Auth Code ke saath.
Aapka backend us Auth Code aur apne private Server Secret ko wapas Google ko bhejta hai. Sab kuch theek hone pe Google ek Access Token deta hai jisko use karke aap finally us user ka email fetch kar lete ho.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • OAuth is an Authorization framework, not explicitly an Authentication protocol (though OpenID Connect is built on top of it for AuthN).
  • The app NEVER sees the user's password.
  • The flow uses an intermediary Authorization Code which is swapped securely server-to-server for the final Access Token to prevent token leakage in the browser.
Q71

Why should passwords never be encrypted, only hashed?

▼

Deep Dive: WebSockets vs Server-Sent Events (SSE)

Standard HTTP is Unidirectional and Request-Driven. The client must ask the server for data; the server cannot proactively send data to the client. For real-time applications (chat apps, live sports scores), this is inefficient because the client has to constantly ping the server asking "Any updates?" (Short Polling).

WebSockets:

Upgrades an HTTP connection into a persistent, Bi-Directional, Full-Duplex TCP connection. Both the Client and the Server can push messages to each other simultaneously at any time. It has very low latency but is complex to scale because stateful connections must be maintained on load balancers.

Server-Sent Events (SSE):

A standard HTTP connection that stays open. It is strictly Uni-Directional (Server to Client). The client asks for a stream, and the server continuously pushes text data down that pipe. It has built-in automatic reconnection, unlike WebSockets.

Real-World Analogy:

HTTP Polling: A kid in the backseat asking "Are we there yet?" every 5 seconds. Highly annoying and wastes energy.

WebSockets: A two-way phone call. Both people can talk and listen at the exact same time. Best for Chat Apps or Multiplayer Games.

Server-Sent Events: A Radio Broadcast. The radio station (Server) continuously pushes music to you. You (Client) can only listen; you cannot talk back through the radio. Best for Live Stock Tickers or News Feeds.

Hinglish Explanation:
Normal HTTP me jab tak frontend request na bheje, backend kuch nahi de sakta. Live apps me ye problem karta hai.
WebSockets ek khuli hui two-way sadak hai. Frontend aur backend dono ek doosre ko bina wait kiye data bhej sakte hain. Ye chat applications ke liye best hai.
SSE (Server-Sent Events) ek one-way sadak hai. Isme connection khula rehta hai, par sirf backend data bhej sakta hai (jaise live cricket score). Iska fayda ye hai ki agar internet jaye, toh ye khud reconnect ho jata hai, jabki WebSockets me reconnect ka code khud likhna padta hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • WebSockets: Bi-directional, Full-duplex over TCP. Use for Chat, Gaming, Collaborative editing.
  • SSE: Uni-directional (Server to Client) over standard HTTP. Use for Live Feeds, Stock Tickers, Notifications.
  • SSE supports automatic reconnection and standard HTTP load balancing out of the box. WebSockets require specialized infrastructure (like Redis Pub/Sub) to scale across multiple servers.
Q72

Difference between sync and async I/O in Node.

▼

Deep Dive: Node.js Clusters & Worker Threads

Node.js is famous for being Single-Threaded (V8 executes your JS on one main thread). If you run a standard Node.js app on a massive 32-core server, it will only use 1 core and the other 31 cores will sit completely idle. To utilize all CPU power, Node provides two distinct modules: Cluster and Worker Threads.

Cluster Module (Scaling Servers):

This module spins up multiple completely independent instances (processes) of your entire Node application—usually one per CPU core. These processes share the exact same server port (e.g., Port 3000). The OS acts as a load balancer, distributing incoming HTTP requests across the workers. If one process crashes due to a bug, the others keep running fine.

Worker Threads (Heavy Math):

This module creates actual threads inside a single Node process. Unlike Clusters (which don't share memory), Worker Threads can share memory using SharedArrayBuffer. This is used strictly when you need to perform heavy, CPU-intensive tasks (like Video Processing or massive math calculations) without freezing the main Event Loop.

Real-World Analogy:

Cluster: Opening 4 entirely separate Burger King restaurants on the same street. Each has its own manager, own kitchen, and own inventory. If one restaurant burns down, the other 3 keep selling burgers. (Great for handling lots of separate web requests).

Worker Threads: Inside ONE Burger King, hiring 4 extra cooks to work in the same kitchen. They share the same fridge and the same manager. (Great for working together to cook one massive 100-patty burger really fast).

Hinglish Explanation:
Node.js single-threaded hone ki wajah se by default server ke saare CPU cores use nahi karta.
Cluster module lagane se Node aapki app ki multiple copies bana deta hai (har CPU core pe ek). Saari copies ek hi port sun rahi hoti hain. Agar 1000 users aaye, toh load sabme barabar bant jata hai.
Worker Threads thode alag hain. Ye tab use hote hain jab aapko koi heavy math ya image processing karni ho. Ye ek hi app ke andar naye workers banate hain jo aapas me memory share kar sakte hain, jisse main thread hang nahi hota.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Cluster: Multi-processing. Copies the entire app. Does NOT share memory. Best for scaling web servers to handle more HTTP requests.
  • Worker Threads: Multi-threading. Shares memory (via SharedArrayBuffer). Best for CPU-intensive tasks (image resizing, cryptography).
  • In production, modern developers rarely write Cluster code manually; they use Process Managers like PM2 or container orchestrators like Kubernetes.
Q73

What causes Node app to crash?

▼

Deep Dive: Redis & In-Memory Caching

Traditional databases (like PostgreSQL or MongoDB) store data on the server's Hard Drive or SSD. While SSDs are fast, they still require I/O operations which take milliseconds. In massive scale applications, doing this for every single request is too slow and will crash the database.

Redis (Remote Dictionary Server) is an In-Memory Database. It stores all of its data directly in the server's RAM. Accessing RAM is orders of magnitude faster (microseconds) than accessing an SSD. It primarily acts as a Key-Value store.

How Caching Works (The Cache-Aside Pattern):

  1. User requests "Top 10 Movies".
  2. Backend checks Redis: "Do you have 'top_movies' in RAM?"
  3. If YES (Cache Hit): Backend returns data instantly. Database is never touched.
  4. If NO (Cache Miss): Backend queries the slow Database, gets the 10 movies, saves a copy into Redis with a Time-To-Live (TTL) of 1 hour, and returns the data to the user.

Real-World Analogy:

Database: The massive City Library. It has every book ever written, but you have to walk 10 blocks, search the index, and find the aisle to get your book. (Slow, but permanent).

Redis: Your backpack. You take the most important book from the library and put it in your backpack. Whenever you need it, you reach in and get it instantly. If it gets heavy or old (TTL expires), you throw it out. (Blazing fast, but temporary memory).

Hinglish Explanation:
Normal Database data ko hard drive me save karta hai. Agar Amazon pe 1 lakh log ek sath "iPhone 15" search karein, toh DB baar baar hard drive ghoomayega aur server crash ho jayega.
Redis ek In-Memory database hai, ye data ko direct RAM me store karta hai jo ki hard drive se hazaro guna fast hota hai. Toh hum kya karte hain ki ek baar DB se iPhone ka data nikal ke Redis me 1 ghante ke liye save kar dete hain (ise Caching kehte hain). Ab jo naye 99,999 log aayenge, unko result microseconds me Redis se mil jayega aur original DB pe koi load nahi padega.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • Redis stores data in RAM, making read/write operations extremely fast (sub-millisecond).
  • Used for Caching, Session Storage (replacing JWTs if needed), and Pub/Sub (for WebSockets).
  • TTL (Time To Live): A critical concept. Cached data must expire automatically so users don't see stale, outdated data.
Q74

How do you handle global errors?

▼

Deep Dive: SQL vs NoSQL

The choice between SQL (Relational) and NoSQL (Non-Relational) dictates how your application structures and scales its data.

  • SQL (PostgreSQL, MySQL): Data is stored in strict, predefined Tables with Rows and Columns. Relationships between tables (Foreign Keys) are strictly enforced. Best for complex queries, financial transactions, and structured data.
  • NoSQL (MongoDB, DynamoDB): Data is stored in flexible JSON-like Documents. There is no strict schema; Document A can have 5 fields, while Document B in the same collection has 10 entirely different fields. Best for rapid development, unstructured data, and massive horizontal scaling.

Scaling Differences:

SQL databases generally scale Vertically (Buy a bigger, more expensive server with more RAM/CPU). NoSQL databases generally scale Horizontally (Buy 5 cheap, small servers and distribute the documents across them natively via Sharding).

Real-World Analogy:

SQL: An Excel Spreadsheet. Every column has a specific header (Name, Age, Email). If you want to add a user who only has a Phone Number, you still have to leave the Name and Email columns blank. Very organized, but rigid.

NoSQL: A folder full of Word Documents. One document might be a 5-page essay, the other might just be a grocery list. You can throw anything in the folder without defining a structure first. Very flexible, but requires you to manage the chaos yourself in your application code.

Hinglish Explanation:
SQL (MySQL/Postgres) me data Tables me save hota hai. Isme strict rules hote hain. Agar 5 columns define kiye hain, toh naya data aate waqt unhi 5 columns me jana chahiye. Ye banking apps ke liye best hai jaha galti ki koi gunjaish nahi hoti.
NoSQL (MongoDB) me data JSON files (Documents) me save hota hai. Isme koi fixed structure nahi hota. Ek user ke pas 2 fields ho sakti hain, dusre ke pas 10 alag fields ho sakti hain. Ye chat apps ya social media ke liye best hai jaha features jaldi jaldi change hote hain aur flexibility chahiye hoti hai.

🎯 Interview Key Takeaways

  • SQL: Strict schema, Relational (JOINs), ACID compliant, Vertically scales.
  • NoSQL: Dynamic schema, Non-relational (Nested data), Horizontally scales (Sharding).
  • Choose SQL for structured data with heavy relationships (E-commerce orders/inventory). Choose NoSQL for rapidly changing data or massive write-heavy logs (IoT data, Chat logs).
Q75

What is rate limiting and why is it needed?

▼

Deep Dive: ACID Properties in Databases

ACID is a set of guiding principles that guarantee database transactions are processed reliably. It is the cornerstone of Relational (SQL) databases, ensuring that even in the event of a power failure or server crash, your data is never left in a corrupted state.

Imagine a Transaction where you are transferring $100 from Account A to Account B.

  • Atomicity (All or Nothing): The transaction is a single unit. Either BOTH steps happen (deduct $100 from A, add $100 to B), or NEITHER happens. If the server crashes after deducting from A but before adding to B, the database automatically "Rolls back" A's money.
  • Consistency: The database must move from one valid state to another valid state. It enforces all rules (like foreign keys and constraints). If Account A only has $50, the database rejects the $100 transfer because it violates the "No negative balances" rule.
  • Isolation: If two different users try to transfer money out of Account A at the exact same millisecond, the database puts them in an invisible queue. The transactions don't interfere with each other; it processes them sequentially as if they happened one after the other.
  • Durability: Once a transaction is committed and the database says "Success", that data is permanently saved to the hard drive. Even if you pull the power plug 1 millisecond later, the data will still be there when the server reboots.

Real-World Analogy:

Buying a Coffee.

Atomicity: You give $5, you get Coffee. If the machine breaks halfway, you get your $5 back. You never lose $5 and get nothing.

Consistency: The machine only accepts valid currency. It rejects Monopoly money.

Isolation: You and another guy try to tap your credit cards on the reader at the same time. The machine only reads one first, processes it, and then reads the second.

Durability: The receipt is printed in permanent ink. The shop owner can't claim you didn't pay if the power goes out.

Hinglish Explanation:
